\subsection{Region specification and boundary role compression}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{RegionSpec} stores the selected sibling-cone cut. \code{build\_\allowbreak{}sibling\_\allowbreak{}regions} chooses an LCA, two adjacent child cones, a boundary consisting of the LCA plus frontier nodes, and an interior inside the two cones. \code{boundary\_\allowbreak{}role\_\allowbreak{}basis} builds finite role columns and \code{compress\_\allowbreak{}response} returns \(Q^T\Lambda_BQ\).

\begin{definition}[Boundary role compression]
For a selected region \(R\), let \(Q_R\) be the matrix whose columns are the surviving Gram-Schmidt role vectors. The compressed response is
\begin{equation}
  \Lambda_R^{\rm role}=Q_R^T\Lambda_BQ_R.
  \label{eq:boundary-role-compression}
\end{equation}
The current symmetric Schur rows use the role names \code{C\_lca}, \code{B1\_front}, and \code{B2\_front} in the accepted compressed basis.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Role compression is a finite congruence]
For an accepted Schur response and a returned role basis \(Q_R\), the compressed response is the finite matrix product \(Q_R^T\Lambda_BQ_R\).
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The objects are \code{boundary\_\allowbreak{}role\_\allowbreak{}basis} and \code{compress\_\allowbreak{}response}. \textbf{Step 1.} Role indicator columns are built from boundary node lists. \textbf{Step 2.} Modified Gram-Schmidt discards columns whose residual norm is below tolerance. \textbf{Step 3.} The returned \(Q_R\) is used in the congruence of Equation~\eqref{eq:boundary-role-compression}. \textbf{Counter-check.} If all role columns vanish numerically, the code supplies a constant fallback column. \textbf{Conclusion.} The role layer changes finite coordinates on the selected boundary; it does not add a new response solve.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Directed response and S/A decomposition}
\paragraph{Code map.} The module \code{core/directed\_\allowbreak{}dtn.py} constructs directed local matrices and directed Schur responses without pair-averaging the edge dictionary and without final Schur symmetrization. The whole-network F1/F2 scalar has already been introduced; this subsection uses a selected boundary/interior cut.

\begin{definition}[Directed local response matrix]
For directed weights \(W_{ij}\), the out-degree sign relation is
\begin{equation}
  K_{ii}=\sum_j W_{ij},\qquad K_{ij}=-W_{ij}\quad(i\ne j).
  \label{eq:directed-local-response-matrix}
\end{equation}
The in-degree sign relation applies the same formula to \(W^T\). The open-grounded response keeps external out-load on the diagonal; the closed-internal response removes this external diagonal load; the environment-port response represents outside load by one additional finite boundary port.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Symmetric/skew split]
For a directed response matrix \(\Lambda\), the finite split is
\begin{equation}
  S=\frac12(\Lambda+\Lambda^T),\qquad A=\frac12(\Lambda-\Lambda^T).
  \label{eq:symmetric-skew-response-split}
\end{equation}
The row families record \(\|S\|_F\), \(\|A\|_F\), \(\|A\|_F/\|\Lambda\|_F\), symmetry residuals, and the signed F1/F2 plane scalar read from \(A\).
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Directed response separates symmetric and skew finite parts]
For every generated directed response row in the stated command, the returned matrix decomposes as \(\Lambda=S+A\), with \(S^T=S\) and \(A^T=-A\) up to the recorded floating-point residuals.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The object is the matrix returned by \code{directed\_\allowbreak{}schur\_\allowbreak{}response}. \textbf{Step 1.} The directed local matrix is built from ordered edge weights. \textbf{Step 2.} Schur elimination is applied without symmetrizing \(\Lambda\). \textbf{Step 3.} \code{split\_\allowbreak{}symmetric\_\allowbreak{}skew} forms \(S\) and \(A\) according to Equation~\eqref{eq:symmetric-skew-response-split}. \textbf{Counter-check.} The row family records separate open-grounded, closed-internal, and environment-port rows; row-sum residuals differ across these finite accounting choices. \textbf{Conclusion.} The selected-cut directed layer supplies finite symmetric and skew matrices, not a continuum operator.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Finite J comparison on an S/A support}
\paragraph{Code map.} The function \code{j\_\allowbreak{}finite\_\allowbreak{}object\_\allowbreak{}from\_\allowbreak{}SA} reads a symmetric matrix \(S\) and a skew matrix \(A\). It restricts to the positive spectral support of \(S\), builds \(J_{\rm raw}=S^{-1/2}AS^{-1/2}\), extracts a polar factor by singular value decomposition, and records the residual for \(J^2=-P\) on the non-zero skew support.

\begin{definition}[Finite support comparison]
Let \(P_S\) be the positive spectral support selected from \(S\). On that finite support the code computes
\begin{equation}
  J_{\rm raw}=S^{-1/2}AS^{-1/2}.
  \label{eq:finite-j-raw}
\end{equation}
If the skew support has rank at least two, the SVD block defines a polar partial isometry \(J=W_kV_k^T\) on the non-zero skew support. With \(P=W_kW_k^T\), the code compares \(J^2\) with \(-P\) through \(\|J^2+P\|_F/\|P\|_F\). Unless the skew rank equals the full positive-support dimension, \(J\) is not asserted to be orthogonal on the whole \(S\)-support.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Finite J comparison is support-restricted]
The returned \code{available} field is one only when the positive support of \(S\) and the non-zero skew support of \(A\) have sufficient finite rank for the polar comparison.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The object is the dictionary returned by \code{j\_\allowbreak{}finite\_\allowbreak{}object\_\allowbreak{}from\_\allowbreak{}SA}. \textbf{Step 1.} The code diagonalizes the symmetric part of \(S\) and selects eigenvectors above tolerance. \textbf{Step 2.} It forms \(J_{\rm raw}\) on that support according to Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-j-raw}. \textbf{Step 3.} It computes an SVD of \(J_{\rm raw}\), retains singular directions above tolerance, and constructs the partial isometry and its range projector. \textbf{Counter-check.} If the positive support rank or skew support rank is below two, the function returns \code{available=0} with a reason string. Even skew rank is recorded but is not used as a separate acceptance gate. \textbf{Conclusion.} The finite \(J\) row tests \(J^2\simeq-P\) on the resolved non-zero skew support inside a selected matrix problem; it does not establish \(J^2=-I\) on a larger carrier.
\end{proof}

\caption{Generated finite-J comparison rows under the current growth rule.}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Row family} & \textbf{Rows} & \textbf{Readout}\\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Row family} & \textbf{Rows} & \textbf{Readout}\\
\midrule
\endhead

\subsection{Finite response algebra and GNS quotient audit}\label{sec:finite-response-gns-audit}
\paragraph{Code map.} The module \code{core/response\_\allowbreak{}algebra.py} defines \code{AlgebraBasis}, finite closure under multiplication and transpose, response-generated generator systems, density matrices, state Gram matrices, tolerance-resolved quotient coordinates, and full-basis left-action audits. The module \code{derived\_\allowbreak{}quantities/gns\_\allowbreak{}cyclicity\_\allowbreak{}controls.py} supplies independent positive and negative ambient-support cyclicity controls.

\begin{definition}[Finite response algebra closure]
For a finite generator list \(G_1,\ldots,G_r\subset M_n(\mathbb R)\), \code{algebra\_\allowbreak{}closure} builds a numerically Frobenius-orthonormal matrix basis by repeated multiplication and transposition. A trial matrix is sequentially projected against the current basis and inserted only when the remaining Frobenius norm exceeds the algebra tolerance. Because the maintained basis is normalized by the same insertion routine, this is an orthogonal-projection residual, not a separate general least-squares solve. The loop stops when no new direction is inserted, the full matrix dimension is reached, or the iteration cap is exhausted.
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Finite GNS Gram data and quotient coordinates]
Given a numerically orthonormal algebra basis \(\{A_i\}_{i=1}^m\) and a density matrix \(D\), the implemented state Gram matrix is
\begin{equation}
  G_{ij}=\operatorname{tr}(D A_i^T A_j).
  \label{eq:finite-gns-gram-matrix}
\end{equation}
Let \(G=U\operatorname{diag}(\lambda)U^T\) and retain the eigenvalues satisfying \(\lambda_i>\max(\varepsilon_{\rm gns},\varepsilon_{\rm gns}\max(1,\lambda_{\max}))\). A coefficient vector \(c\in\mathbb R^m\) is represented on the finite quotient support by
\[
  q(c)=\operatorname{diag}(\sqrt{\lambda_+})U_+^T c.
\]
For the identity coefficient vector \(c_I\), \(\Omega=q(c_I)\). If \(C_i\) is the coefficient matrix of left multiplication by \(A_i\), the explicit cyclic-span test is
\begin{equation}
  r_{\rm cyc}=\operatorname{rank}\bigl[q(C_1c_I)\;\cdots\;q(C_mc_I)\bigr],
  \qquad \text{cyclic exactly when }r_{\rm cyc}=\operatorname{rank}G.
  \label{eq:finite-gns-cyclic-rank}
\end{equation}
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Full-basis finite GNS audit]
For each finite algebra/density pair, \code{measurement\_\allowbreak{}gns} computes the Gram support and audits every algebra-basis element: left-closure, transpose-adjoint compatibility, stability of the Gram nullspace under left multiplication, the identity-span residual, the equality \(\pi(A_i)\Omega=[A_i]\), and the cyclic rank in Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-gns-cyclic-rank}.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The object is \code{GNSData}. \textbf{Step 1.} Compute Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-gns-gram-matrix} and its tolerance-resolved positive support. \textbf{Step 2.} Project the identity into the algebra basis and record its span residual and norm. \textbf{Step 3.} For every basis element, compute the left-multiplication coefficient matrix and the corresponding quotient representation. \textbf{Step 4.} Compare the represented transpose with the Euclidean adjoint, test images of Gram-null vectors, and compare \(q(C_ic_I)\) with the direct class \(q(e_i)\). \textbf{Step 5.} Compute the rank of all action-on-\(\Omega\) columns. \textbf{Counter-check.} The separate file \src{finite\_gns\_cyclicity\_controls.csv} includes full-algebra positive controls and proper-subalgebra negative controls; hence the cyclicity criterion is not the tautology \(\operatorname{rank}G\le m\). \textbf{Conclusion.} The row reports a finite left-regular GNS quotient audit over the full stored basis. No Hilbert completion, von Neumann closure, or infinite cyclicity statement is made.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Reproduction command.}
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg
PYTHONPATH=src python3 run_cnna.py \
  --branching 3 \
  --levels 3 4 \
  --modes linear log saturating \
  --states live record sym \
  --max-regions 1 \
  --max-boundary 600 \
  --max-interior 900 \
  --directed-frontier-cap 6 \
  --algebra-tol 1e-10 \
  --algebra-max-iter 8 \
  --gns-tol 1e-10 \
  --growth-schedule layer_batch \
  --outdir outputs/algebra_gns_state_entropy
\end{verbatim}

\subsection{Regional response object and local support algebras}
\paragraph{Code map.} The modules \code{core/region\_\allowbreak{}response.py} and \code{core/algebra\_\allowbreak{}support.py} build regional response containers, positive-support densities, role-coordinate maps, role support projectors, local generator systems, local algebra closures, joined algebras, commutator metrics, GNS support ranks, commutant bases, finite Tomita-star metrics, and split residuals.

\begin{definition}[Local support algebra]
For a selected regional response, the code constructs role supports as index subsets of the finite boundary-role coordinate system. The local support algebra is the finite matrix algebra generated by the response-derived matrices after restriction to the selected role support.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Local algebra rows are finite matrix comparisons]
The local algebra rows compare finite generated matrix algebras inside explicitly chosen ambient coordinates.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The objects are role-support projectors, restricted generator lists, and algebra bases. \textbf{Step 1.} Select role coordinates from the regional response object. \textbf{Step 2.} Restrict the generator matrices to the selected support. \textbf{Step 3.} Build finite algebra bases by the same closure routine as above. \textbf{Step 4.} Compare inclusions, commutators, joins, commutants, cyclic spans, and split dimensions by finite ranks and finite norms. \textbf{Counter-check.} A non-zero residual is recorded as a finite matrix discrepancy in the selected coordinate system. \textbf{Conclusion.} The row families describe finite algebraic relations for the selected regional response.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Reproduction command.}
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg

  --branching 3 \
  --levels 4 \
  --modes linear log saturating \
  --states live record sym \
  --max-regions 1 \
  --max-boundary 600 \
  --max-interior 900 \

\end{verbatim}

\subsection{Compressed carrier/GNS state and projective distribution}
\paragraph{Code map.} The module \code{core/compressed\_\allowbreak{}state.py} builds a finite compressed state object over role/address keys. The corresponding row functions in \code{derived\_\allowbreak{}quantities/compressed\_\allowbreak{}state\_\allowbreak{}projection\_\allowbreak{}consistency.py} compare direct compression with projected compression at lower target levels.

\begin{definition}[Compressed carrier state]
A compressed carrier state maps terminal or boundary atoms to finite role/address keys and sums the associated weights. The object contains the compressed key set, finite state weights, trace weights, response-energy weights, and residuals for the projected response density.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Compressed projectivity is a route comparison]
For target levels \(k<\ell\), the compressed projectivity rows compare direct compression at level \(k\) with the push-forward of compression at level \(\ell\).
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The object is a pair of finite compressed distributions over role/address keys. \textbf{Step 1.} Build the compressed state at the finer target level. \textbf{Step 2.} Push it forward by address truncation. \textbf{Step 3.} Build the coarser compressed state directly. \textbf{Step 4.} Compare the two routes by total variation and sector profiles. \textbf{Counter-check.} A non-zero value records finite route dependence for the implemented compression. \textbf{Conclusion.} The row is a finite compatibility measurement for the compression map.
\end{proof}

A refined role basis is a finite boundary-coordinate basis obtained by subdividing frontier role groups into named shells or chunks. It replaces a coarse role column by several normalized columns whose supports partition the original role support.

\subsection{Entropy functionals and record/live entropy distances}\label{sec:entropy-functionals-record-live}
\paragraph{Code map.} The module \code{core/entropy\_\allowbreak{}quantities.py} defines finite entropy, finite relative entropy, Jensen--Shannon divergence, and record/live entropy metrics. The generated object-check rows evaluate these functions on densities attached to regional response and local algebra data. The same finite entropy and divergence functions are read again in Section~\ref{sec:live-record-backreaction-current} through the record/live row families.

\begin{definition}[Tolerance-resolved finite entropy]
For a symmetric positive semidefinite density \(D\), let
\[
  \tau_D=\max\!\left(\varepsilon_{\rm gns},\varepsilon_{\rm gns}\max(1,\lambda_{\max}(D))\right).
\]
The function \code{von\_\allowbreak{}neumann\_\allowbreak{}entropy\_\allowbreak{}no\_\allowbreak{}epsilon} computes
\begin{equation}
  S_{\tau_D}(D)=-\sum_{\lambda_i>\tau_D}\lambda_i\log\lambda_i.
  \label{eq:finite-von-neumann-entropy}
\end{equation}
No artificial floor is inserted inside the logarithm, but eigenvalues at or below the scale-aware cutoff are treated as numerical zero. Relative support inclusion is likewise tolerance-resolved; the routine returns an infinite value when the resolved support of \(\rho\) is not contained in that of \(\sigma\).
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[The entropy routines expose their numerical support rule]
The entropy functions do not add an epsilon floor to force finite logarithms. They first resolve spectral support at the declared tolerance and test support leakage before the logarithmic trace expression is evaluated.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The objects are the spectra and support projectors computed in \code{entropy\_\allowbreak{}quantities.py}. \textbf{Step 1.} Entropy sums eigenvalues above \(\tau_D\). \textbf{Step 2.} Relative entropy compares the trace leakage of \(\rho\) outside the resolved support projector of \(\sigma\) with a scale-aware tolerance. \textbf{Step 3.} Jensen--Shannon divergence uses the mixture density and the same support routine. \textbf{Counter-check.} Eigenvalues below the cutoff are not distinguished from exact zero, so the computation is not an exact symbolic-support calculation. \textbf{Conclusion.} The row values keep the implemented tolerance-resolved support relation visible without logarithmic epsilon flooring.
\end{proof}

\newpage
\section{Finite structural results and exact proof boundary}\label{sec:finite-structural-results}
This section separates mathematical statements that follow from the finite definitions from numerical controls and from stronger claims that the present source does not establish. Exact statements are formulated over finite-dimensional real vector spaces and exact arithmetic. Their Python realization is additionally subject to the declared binary64 environment and tolerance rules. A passing control row checks the implementation on explicit finite cases; it is not substituted for the proof.

\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.25\linewidth}>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.18\linewidth}>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.47\linewidth}}
\caption{Status of the six finite structural statements.}\label{tab:finite-structural-status}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Statement} & \textbf{Status} & \textbf{Scope}\\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Statement} & \textbf{Status} & \textbf{Scope}\\
\midrule
\endhead
Finite growth well-definedness & exact finite theorem & fixed admissible inputs, fixed sibling linearization, and the explicitly specified update rule.\\\hline
Symmetric Schur positivity & exact conditional theorem & non-negative symmetrized conductances; invertible interior block. Positive definiteness requires explicit grounding.\\\hline
Finite GNS quotient & exact finite theorem & a unital finite-dimensional real transpose-closed matrix algebra and a positive density matrix.\\\hline
Global orientation reversal & exact conditional obstruction & sign-even data cannot distinguish the pair obtained by simultaneous reversal of the skew orientation.\\\hline
Backreaction scalar separation & exact non-implication plus generated witness & distinguishes the matrix current, its norm, a difference of norms, and one oriented matrix coefficient.\\\hline
Interlevel handoff & exact at address/carrier level; conditional for boundary matrices; failed for a strict operator-algebra handoff & prefix maps and uniform terminal carriers compose exactly. Current response/algebra maps retain non-zero discrepancies.\\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

The implementation controls for this section are generated by
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg
PYTHONPATH=src python3 \
  package_consistency/check_finite_structure_theorems.py \
  --write-csv docs/FINITE_STRUCTURE_THEOREM_CONTROLS.csv
\end{verbatim}
The six control rows pass in the documented environment. Their role is to detect implementation regressions in the finite hypotheses used below.

\subsection{Well-definedness of the finite growth construction}
Let
\[
  \mathcal W_{b,L}=\bigcup_{d=0}^{L}\{0,\ldots,b-1\}^{d}
\]
be the finite address set, including the empty word at depth zero. Besides \((b,L,m,\mathfrak s)\), the source admits a fixed sibling linearization \(\ell\in\{\code{rank},\code{reverse\_rank}\}\). The reference calculations use \(\ell=\code{rank}\).

\begin{theorem}[Finite growth well-definedness]\label{thm:finite-growth-well-defined}
For every admissible tuple
\[
 b\ge2,\qquad L\ge0,\qquad
 m\in\{\code{linear},\code{log},\code{saturating}\},\qquad
 \mathfrak s\in\{\code{slot\_step},\code{layer\_batch}\},
\]
and every fixed sibling linearization \(\ell\), the exact finite rule determines a unique tuple
\[
  \mathcal G_{b,L,m,\mathfrak s,\ell}
  =\bigl(V,E_{\rm record},E_{\rm live},g,\mathcal E\bigr).
\]
There is exactly one node \(v_a\) for each \(a\in\mathcal W_{b,L}\), with parent \(v_{a^-}\) for every non-empty word \(a\). In particular,
\begin{equation}
  |V|=|\mathcal W_{b,L}|=\sum_{d=0}^{L}b^d=\frac{b^{L+1}-1}{b-1}.
  \label{eq:finite-growth-cardinality-theorem}
\end{equation}
The event list, record-edge dictionary, live-edge dictionary, and node loads are finite and uniquely determined by the fixed inputs and rule equations.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The proof is by induction on depth. At depth zero, \code{\_birth\_root\_from\_empty} creates the unique empty-address node. Assume that after depth \(d\) the nodes are exactly the words of lengths at most \(d\), with one node per address. Every depth-\(d\) word has precisely the unoccupied ranks \(0,\ldots,b-1\); concatenating a rank produces a distinct word of length \(d+1\), and every word of length \(d+1\) has exactly one such predecessor. The ordered slot list is a deterministic function of \(\ell\). For \code{slot\_step}, each growth step is evaluated and committed in that order. For \code{layer\_batch}, all step data at one depth are evaluated from the same pre-commit state and are then committed in the same deterministic slot order; the subsequent sibling relation loop is ordered by sibling rank. Every weight and load update is a finite arithmetic expression in the already determined state. Duplicate addresses are rejected explicitly. Hence the construction terminates after \(L\) depth loops and is unique. Counting the words gives Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-growth-cardinality-theorem}.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Implementation scope]
The theorem concerns the exact rule. The control file replays 96 finite configurations covering \(b\in\{2,3\}\), \(L\in\{0,1,2,3\}\), all three modes, both schedules, and both sibling linearizations. All 96 paired replays are exactly equal in the documented environment; address, parent, and finiteness checks have zero failures. This does not claim bitwise equality across different floating-point libraries or compiler builds.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Positivity of the symmetric finite response}
For one state, let
\[
 c_{ij}=\frac{w_{ij}+w_{ji}}{2}\ge0
\]
be the undirected conductance used by the symmetric response route, and let \(L_C\) be its weighted graph Laplacian. The open-grounded local matrix constructed by \code{schur\_response\_from\_model} is the principal block \(K=(L_C)_{S,S}\) for \(S=B\sqcup I\); edges from \(S\) to omitted vertices remain in its diagonal. The proof below is the finite quadratic-form proof of the standard Schur-complement positivity statement \cite{HornJohnson2013,Zhang2005}.

\begin{theorem}[Finite Schur positivity]\label{thm:finite-schur-positivity}
The local matrix \(K\) is positive semidefinite. If \(K_{II}\) is invertible, then it is positive definite and the exact Schur response
\begin{equation}
  \Lambda_B=K_{BB}-K_{BI}K_{II}^{-1}K_{IB}
  \label{eq:schur-positivity-response}
\end{equation}
is positive semidefinite. If every connected component of the conductance graph induced on \(S\) has a positive-conductance edge to the omitted complement, then \(K\) and \(\Lambda_B\) are positive definite. If no such grounding is present and a whole connected component is retained, constants on that component can remain in the kernel; positivity then holds on the quotient by that kernel.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
For \(x\in\mathbb R^S\), extension by zero outside \(S\) gives
\begin{equation}
 x^TKx
 =\sum_{\substack{i<j\\ i,j\in S}}c_{ij}(x_i-x_j)^2
  +\sum_{\substack{i\in S\\j\notin S}}c_{ij}x_i^2\ge0.
 \label{eq:grounded-laplacian-energy}
\end{equation}
Thus \(K\succeq0\). A nonsingular symmetric principal block of a positive-semidefinite matrix is positive definite, so the accepted exact interior block satisfies \(K_{II}\succ0\). For any boundary vector \(y\), minimize the quadratic form of \(K\) over interior vectors. The unique minimizer is \(z=-K_{II}^{-1}K_{IB}y\), and the minimum equals \(y^T\Lambda_By\). It is therefore non-negative. Equation~\eqref{eq:grounded-laplacian-energy} vanishes only when \(x\) is constant on each selected conductance component and zero on every component touching the omitted complement. The stated grounding condition forces \(x=0\), hence \(K\succ0\); the Schur complement of a positive-definite block matrix is positive definite.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Directed limitation]
The theorem applies only to the symmetrized conductance/Laplacian route. A directed response matrix need not be symmetric or positive semidefinite; only its symmetric part can be subjected to a separate support test. The control file evaluates 36 open-grounded symmetric responses over both schedules, all three modes, two sibling regions, and the states live, record, and sym. Every response is symmetric and positive definite in that sample; the smallest observed eigenvalue is approximately \(1.2315\times10^{-1}\). The proof, rather than this sample, supplies the positive-semidefinite statement under the theorem's hypotheses.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Exact finite GNS quotient}
The numerical routine in Section~\ref{sec:finite-response-gns-audit} is an audit of an exact finite-dimensional construction. The following real finite-matrix specialization is proved directly and uses the standard GNS quotient principle \cite{GelfandNaimark1943,Segal1947}; it is independent of the tolerance-resolved eigensolver.

\begin{theorem}[Finite real GNS construction]\label{thm:finite-real-gns}
Let \(\mathcal A\subseteq M_n(\mathbb R)\) be a finite-dimensional unital algebra closed under transpose, and let \(D\succeq0\) with \(\operatorname{tr}D=1\). Define
\[
 \varphi_D(X)=\operatorname{tr}(DX),\qquad
 \langle X,Y\rangle_D=\varphi_D(X^TY),
\]
and
\begin{equation}
 \mathcal N_D=\{X\in\mathcal A:\varphi_D(X^TX)=0\}.
 \label{eq:finite-gns-nullspace-theorem}
\end{equation}
Then \(\mathcal N_D\) is a left ideal, the quotient
\[
 \mathcal H_D=\mathcal A/\mathcal N_D
\]
is a finite-dimensional real inner-product space, and
\[
 \pi_D(A)[X]=[AX]
\]
defines a transpose representation satisfying \(\pi_D(A)^*=\pi_D(A^T)\). The vector \(\Omega=[I]\) is cyclic because \(\pi_D(A)\Omega=[A]\) for every \(A\in\mathcal A\). For any basis of \(\mathcal A\), the Gram matrix in Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-gns-gram-matrix} satisfies
\begin{equation}
  \operatorname{rank}G=\dim\mathcal H_D,
  \qquad
  \operatorname{nullity}G=\dim\mathcal N_D.
  \label{eq:finite-gns-rank-nullity-theorem}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Since
\[
 \varphi_D(X^TX)
 =\operatorname{tr}(D^{1/2}X^TXD^{1/2})
 =\|XD^{1/2}\|_F^2,
\]
Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-gns-nullspace-theorem} is equivalent to \(XD^{1/2}=0\). Therefore \((AX)D^{1/2}=A(XD^{1/2})=0\), so \(\mathcal N_D\) is a left ideal. The sesquilinear issues of a complex space do not occur here: the real bilinear form descends to a positive-definite inner product on the quotient. Left multiplication is well-defined by the left-ideal property, and
\[
 \langle AX,Y\rangle_D
 =\operatorname{tr}(DX^TA^TY)
 =\langle X,A^TY\rangle_D.
\]
Cyclicity follows immediately from \(\pi_D(A)[I]=[A]\). The Gram matrix is the matrix of the positive semidefinite form on the chosen basis, so its kernel is the coefficient representation of \(\mathcal N_D\) and rank-nullity gives Equation~\eqref{eq:finite-gns-rank-nullity-theorem}.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[What the code still has to check]
The exact theorem assumes a genuinely unital transpose-closed algebra. The generated basis is numerical and may be stopped by an iteration cap. Therefore the implementation separately records saturation, identity-span, multiplication-closure, transpose-adjoint, null-left-ideal, action-on-\(\Omega\), and cyclic-rank residuals. The four explicit controls include cyclic and non-cyclic ambient-support cases; all pass. No completion, irreducibility, factor property, or infinite GNS statement follows.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Orientation reversal and the unresolved absolute sign}
\begin{theorem}[Sign-reversal covariance of the finite polar object]\label{thm:j-sign-reversal}
Let \(S\succeq0\), let \(A^T=-A\), and form the support-restricted object
\[
 J_{\rm raw}(S,A)=S^{-1/2}AS^{-1/2}.
\]
Let \(U(S,A)\) denote its polar partial isometry on the non-zero skew support. Then
\begin{equation}
 J_{\rm raw}(S,-A)=-J_{\rm raw}(S,A),
 \qquad
 U(S,-A)=-U(S,A).
 \label{eq:j-sign-reversal-covariance}
\end{equation}
The singular values, skew rank, support projector, and square relation \(U^2=-P\) are unchanged by this reversal. Consequently, any selection rule using only \(S\) and these sign-even data cannot distinguish the two globally reversed choices.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The first identity is linearity in \(A\). The absolute value of an operator is unchanged by multiplication by \(-1\), so in the polar decomposition \(J_{\rm raw}=U|J_{\rm raw}|\), replacing \(J_{\rm raw}\) by its negative replaces \(U\) by \(-U\) on the same support. All listed diagnostics are functions of \(|J_{\rm raw}|\), the support projector, or \(U^2\), and are therefore invariant.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Relative orientation versus absolute orientation]\label{cor:relative-absolute-orientation}
Once the sibling-slot F2 coordinate is fixed, the finite rows can compare local and global signs relative to that coordinate. Under a simultaneous reversal of every such sign, each relative product is unchanged. The present core therefore supplies relative orientation data but no sign-odd predicate that declares one simultaneous global choice to be the absolute physical sign.
\end{corollary}

\begin{remark}[Conditional nature of the obstruction]
The obstruction does not say that the computed \(J\) is undefined for a given oriented \(A\); Equation~\eqref{eq:j-sign-reversal-covariance} determines it. It says that a model specification invariant under simultaneous orientation reversal cannot turn the two reversed specifications into inequivalent absolute choices without adding or deriving another sign-odd datum. The control error for \(U(S,-A)+U(S,A)\) is \(3.14\times10^{-16}\); singular values and square residuals agree within the declared tolerance.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Separation of backreaction observables}
\begin{proposition}[Backreaction non-implications]\label{prop:backreaction-nonimplications}
For skew matrices \(A_{\rm live}\) and \(A_{\rm record}\), define
\[
 B=A_{\rm live}-A_{\rm record},\qquad
 b=\|B\|_F,\qquad
 \beta=\|A_{\rm live}\|_F-\|A_{\rm record}\|_F,
\]
and \(\omega=F_1^TBF_2\) for fixed vectors \(F_1,F_2\). Then
\[
 B=0\ \Longrightarrow\ b=0,\ \beta=0,\ \omega=0,
\]
but neither \(\beta=0\Rightarrow B=0\) nor \(\beta=0\Rightarrow\omega=0\) is valid in general.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
The forward implications are immediate. For the converse, take
\[
 J_2=\begin{pmatrix}0&-1\\1&0\end{pmatrix},\qquad
 A_{\rm live}=J_2,\qquad A_{\rm record}=-J_2.
\]
Then \(\beta=0\), whereas \(B=2J_2\), \(\|B\|_F=2\sqrt2\), and with \(F_1=e_1,F_2=e_2\), \(\omega=-2\). Thus equality of the two skew norms does not identify the matrices or an oriented coefficient.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Meaning of the generated crossing]\label{cor:beta-crossing-only}
A sign change of the sampled sequence \(\beta_L\) states only that the order of the two skew norms changes between the sampled levels. It does not imply an intermediate zero of a matrix-valued path \(B_L\), because no continuous matrix interpolation is defined, and it does not imply a zero of \(\omega_L\). In the documented \(b=4\), \(L=4,5,6\) rows, \(\beta_L\) changes sign while \(\|B_L\|_F>0\) at every sample and \(\omega_L<0\) throughout.
\end{corollary}

\subsection{Exact prefix handoff}
\begin{theorem}[Address and carrier handoff]\label{thm:prefix-carrier-handoff}
For \(0\le L\le M\le N\), address truncation satisfies
\begin{equation}
  \pi_L\circ\pi_M=\pi_L.
  \label{eq:prefix-map-composition}
\end{equation}
For every non-negative probability distribution \(\mu_N\) on level-\(N\) addresses,
\begin{equation}
  (\pi_L)_*\bigl((\pi_M)_*\mu_N\bigr)=(\pi_L)_*\mu_N.
  \label{eq:probability-pushforward-composition}
\end{equation}
For the uniform terminal carrier \(\Omega_N\) of the full \(b\)-ary tree,
\begin{equation}
  (\pi_L)_*\Omega_N=\Omega_L.
  \label{eq:uniform-carrier-projectivity-theorem}
\end{equation}
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Equation~\eqref{eq:prefix-map-composition} is the associativity of word truncation. Push-forward composition follows by summing over the same address fibers; normalization is inactive because a non-negative probability distribution retains total mass one under each raw push-forward. For Equation~\eqref{eq:uniform-carrier-projectivity-theorem}, every length-\(L\) word has exactly \(b^{N-L}\) length-\(N\) extensions, so all target fibers have equal mass.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Conditional composition of boundary transport matrices]\label{prop:boundary-transport-composition}
Let \(R_{L\leftarrow M}\) be a boundary address-prefix matrix. If every source boundary address maps to the intermediate and target boundary sets, then the \code{fiber\_sum} matrices compose exactly. The \code{fiber\_average} and \code{fiber\_sqrt} matrices also compose when the relevant fiber cardinalities factor uniformly through the intermediate boundary. Under these conditions,
\begin{equation}
  R_{L\leftarrow N}=R_{L\leftarrow M}R_{M\leftarrow N}.
  \label{eq:boundary-transport-matrix-composition}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Each source column has one address-determined target row. For \code{fiber\_sum}, matrix multiplication follows the unique intermediate row and reproduces the direct unit entry. For the two normalized weightings, the composed coefficient is the product of the two inverse fiber factors; the stated factorization condition makes this product equal to the direct inverse fiber factor.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Legacy label projection versus the new carrier map]
The earlier interlevel rows use a least-squares projection between equally named generators in independently reconstructed three-role algebras.  That comparison remains non-multiplicative: its maximum product-morphism residual is~\(1\), its strict star-hom indicator is zero, its maximum GNS Gram transport error is approximately \(6.82\times10^{-1}\), and its conditional-expectation state drift reaches approximately~\(1.46\).  Those rows are retained as a negative control for that particular label-span map.  They no longer exhaust the handoff question, because the following construction introduces a genuinely nested carrier before comparing distinguished response and state data.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Growing address-contrast carriers and non-unital corner embeddings}\label{sec:nested-multiscale-handoff}
\paragraph{Code map.}
\code{core/nested\_multiscale.py} constructs the nested address-coordinate contrast basis, its label inclusion, the corner maps, the resolved support graph, and the common-ambient Schur comparison.  \code{derived\_quantities/nested\_multiscale\_handoff.py} produces the carrier, handoff, composition, state/GNS, finite-\(J\), and common-ambient rows.  The complete control is executed by
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg
python3 package_consistency/check_nested_multiscale_handoff.py \
  --output-dir docs
\end{verbatim}

\begin{definition}[First-order address-contrast carrier]\label{def:first-order-address-carrier}
Fix one sibling-cone region and let the left and right frontier words have relative length~\(h\) below their sibling roots.  Let
\(\eta^{(1)},\ldots,\eta^{(b-1)}\in\R^b\)
be the real Helmert contrasts, an orthonormal basis of the subspace orthogonal to the constant slot vector.  The carrier basis contains the normalized LCA indicator, the normalized constant indicator of each sibling frontier, and, for each side \(\sigma\in\{1,2\}\), address coordinate \(d=0,\ldots,h-1\), and contrast \(r=1,\ldots,b-1\), the vector
\begin{equation}
 q_{\sigma,d,r}(a)
 =
 \begin{cases}
 b^{-(h-1)/2}\,\eta^{(r)}_{a_d},
   & a\text{ belongs to frontier }\sigma,\\
 0,&\text{otherwise}.
 \end{cases}
 \label{eq:first-order-address-contrast}
\end{equation}
The resulting real carrier \(K_{b,h}\) has dimension
\begin{equation}
 d_{b,h}=3+2h(b-1).
 \label{eq:nested-role-dimension}
\end{equation}
Only first-order address-coordinate contrasts are included; products of contrasts at different address coordinates are deliberately omitted.  Thus the carrier grows linearly in~\(h\), rather than exponentially with the full frontier size.
\end{definition}

\begin{theorem}[Exact nested carrier handoff]\label{thm:exact-nested-carrier-handoff}
Let \(L<M\), let \(R_{L\leftarrow M}\) be the \code{fiber\_sqrt} boundary prefix matrix, and put \(V_{M\leftarrow L}=R_{L\leftarrow M}^{T}\).  For complete sibling-frontier fibers, let \(Q_L,Q_M\) be the bases from Definition~\ref{def:first-order-address-carrier}.  Then there is a label-inclusion isometry
\begin{equation}
 T_{M\leftarrow L}:K_L\longrightarrow K_M,
 \qquad T_{M\leftarrow L}^{T}T_{M\leftarrow L}=I_{K_L},
 \label{eq:nested-role-inclusion}
\end{equation}
such that
\begin{equation}
 V_{M\leftarrow L}Q_L=Q_MT_{M\leftarrow L}.
 \label{eq:nested-basis-intertwining}
\end{equation}
Consequently the map
\begin{equation}
 \iota_{M\leftarrow L}(X)
 =T_{M\leftarrow L}XT_{M\leftarrow L}^{T},
 \qquad X\in M_{d_L}(\R),
 \label{eq:nonunital-corner-embedding}
\end{equation}
is an injective transpose-preserving algebra homomorphism.  Its compression
\begin{equation}
 E_{L\leftarrow M}(Y)=T_{M\leftarrow L}^{T}YT_{M\leftarrow L}
 \label{eq:corner-left-inverse}
\end{equation}
satisfies \(E_{L\leftarrow M}\circ\iota_{M\leftarrow L}=\mathrm{id}\).  If \(d_M>d_L\), the embedding is non-unital in the full target matrix algebra:
\begin{equation}
 \iota_{M\leftarrow L}(I_{d_L})
 =P_{M,L}:=T_{M\leftarrow L}T_{M\leftarrow L}^{T}
 \neq I_{d_M}.
 \label{eq:old-role-corner-projector}
\end{equation}
For \(L<M<N\), the inclusions and corner projectors compose through the intermediate level.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Each coarse frontier word has exactly \(b^{M-L}\) fine extensions.  The \code{fiber\_sqrt} coefficient is the inverse square root of this fiber size.  It therefore sends a normalized constant block to the corresponding normalized constant block.  A contrast in Equation~\eqref{eq:first-order-address-contrast} depends only on one address coordinate already present at level~\(L\); every fine extension has the same value on that coordinate, and the square-root fiber normalization again gives the level-\(M\) vector with the same label.  New level-\(M\) columns depend on later address coordinates and are orthogonal to the lifted old columns.  This proves Equations~\eqref{eq:nested-role-inclusion} and~\eqref{eq:nested-basis-intertwining}.  Multiplicativity follows from
\[
 (TXT^T)(TYT^T)=TX(T^TT)YT^T=TXYT^T,
\]
transpose preservation is immediate, and Equation~\eqref{eq:corner-left-inverse} follows from \(T^TT=I\).  Label inclusions compose, hence so do the carrier maps.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Resolved response generation of the carrier algebra]\label{prop:resolved-response-full-algebra}
Let \(\Lambda_L^{\rm ms}=Q_L^T\Lambda_LQ_L\), let \(P_i=e_ie_i^T\) be all coordinate projections of \(K_L\), and define
\begin{equation}
 \mathcal A_L^{\rm ms}
 =\operatorname{alg}^{T}\!\left(\{P_i\}_{i=1}^{d_L}\cup\{\Lambda_L^{\rm ms}\}\right).
 \label{eq:multiscale-response-algebra}
\end{equation}
If the undirected support graph with an edge \(i\!\sim\!j\) whenever
\((\Lambda_L^{\rm ms})_{ij}\neq0\) or \((\Lambda_L^{\rm ms})_{ji}\neq0\)
is connected, then
\begin{equation}
 \mathcal A_L^{\rm ms}=M_{d_L}(\R).
 \label{eq:connected-support-full-algebra}
\end{equation}
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
For a supported directed entry,
\(P_i\Lambda_L^{\rm ms}P_j=(\Lambda_L^{\rm ms})_{ij}E_{ij}\).
Transpose closure supplies the reverse matrix unit.  Multiplying matrix units along a path in the connected support graph produces \(E_{ij}\) for every ordered pair.  The matrix units span \(M_{d_L}(\R)\).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Numerical support qualification]
Equation~\eqref{eq:connected-support-full-algebra} is an exact conditional statement.  The implementation checks its premise on the support resolved at threshold \(10^{-12}\); it does not convert a floating-point entry above threshold into a symbolic non-vanishing theorem.  All nine tested live rows for \(b=2,3,4\) and \(L=4,5,6\) have one connected symmetric and directed support component.  Their carrier dimensions are
\[
7,9,11;\qquad 11,15,19;\qquad 15,21,27,
\]
respectively, and the corresponding full carrier-algebra dimensions reach \(121\), \(361\), and \(729\) at level~6.
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Conditional corner state and scaled GNS identity]\label{prop:conditional-corner-state-gns}
Let \(D_M\) be a normalized positive density on \(K_M\), let \(T=T_{M\leftarrow L}\), and define
\begin{equation}
 C_{M\downarrow L}=T^TD_MT,
 \qquad
 \lambda_{M,L}=\operatorname{tr}(C_{M\downarrow L}),
 \qquad
 D_{M\mid L}=\frac{C_{M\downarrow L}}{\lambda_{M,L}}
 \label{eq:conditional-corner-density}
\end{equation}
whenever \(\lambda_{M,L}>0\).  Then for all \(X,Y\in M_{d_L}(\R)\),
\begin{equation}
 \left\langle\iota_{M\leftarrow L}(X),
                  \iota_{M\leftarrow L}(Y)\right\rangle_{D_M}
 =
 \lambda_{M,L}\,\langle X,Y\rangle_{D_{M\mid L}}.
 \label{eq:scaled-corner-gns-isometry}
\end{equation}
Thus \(\lambda_{M,L}^{-1/2}\iota_{M\leftarrow L}\) induces an isometry between the conditional finite GNS pre-Hilbert spaces.  It induces an isometry from the independently reconstructed level-\(L\) state only if \(D_L=D_{M\mid L}\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Using \(T^TT=I\) and cyclicity of the finite trace,
\[
 \operatorname{tr}\!\left(D_M(TXT^T)^T(TYT^T)\right)
 =\operatorname{tr}\!\left(T^TD_MT X^TY\right)
 =\lambda_{M,L}\operatorname{tr}\!\left(D_{M\mid L}X^TY\right).
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Common-ambient Schur positive control]\label{thm:common-ambient-interlevel-schur}
Fix one fine finite model, one open-grounded symmetric local matrix~\(K_M\), and a coarse retained boundary~\(B_L\).  Let \(C\) be an intermediate retained set containing \(B_L\).  Whenever the required interior blocks are invertible,
\begin{equation}
 \operatorname{Schur}_{B_L}(K_M)
 =
 \operatorname{Schur}_{B_L}\!\left(
   \operatorname{Schur}_{C}(K_M)
 \right).
 \label{eq:common-ambient-schur-associativity}
\end{equation}
This identity compares two elimination orders inside one ambient matrix.  It does not imply equality with the response reconstructed independently from the level-\(L\) growth object.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Both sides are the block Gaussian elimination of the same coordinates of~\(K_M\).  Eliminating the complement of~\(C\) and then \(C\setminus B_L\) gives the same Schur complement as eliminating their union in one step.
\end{proof}

\centering

\end{table}

\begin{corollary}[Record invariance and live backreaction in the common ambient]\label{cor:record-live-common-ambient}
For the tested common-ambient cases \((b,L,M)=(2,4,6),(3,4,6),(4,4,5)\), direct and staged elimination agree to at most \(1.26\times10^{-15}\).  The independently reconstructed record response agrees with the nested record response to at most \(6.67\times10^{-16}\) in the full boundary matrix and \(4.49\times10^{-16}\) after multiscale compression.  The corresponding live compressed drift is non-zero, ranging from \(0.272\) to \(0.657\).  These rows isolate immutable record extension from later live backreaction for the stated finite cases; they are not a general asymptotic theorem.
\end{corollary}

\begin{remark}[What the last arrow now contains]
The growing carrier algebras
\(M_{d_L}(\R)\) and their non-unital corner embeddings are now an exact finite directed system, and the resolved support test shows that the response together with all canonical coordinate projections generates the full carrier algebra in every sampled row.  What remains absent is a natural family of the distinguished objects.  The independently reconstructed responses and densities do not satisfy the corner restriction identities, the independently reconstructed GNS metrics therefore drift, and the finite-\(J\) operators neither intertwine nor preserve the old carrier subspaces.  Consequently the package now separates an exact algebraic carrier handoff from the still-failed response/state/GNS/\(J\) handoff.  A norm completion or physical interpretation of the resulting growing carrier system lies outside the finite scope of this article.
\end{remark}


\paragraph{Why the finite restriction is mathematically informative.}
Finiteness is used here as an audit condition rather than merely as a computational cutoff. It allows the provenance of every algebraic object to be traced back to explicit growth and response data, and it prevents unresolved limit operations from concealing failures of positivity, cyclicity, orientation, multiplicativity, or state compatibility. A property that fails in the finite core is not repaired merely by naming a continuum or operator-algebraic limit; a restoration would require an additional construction and a separate proof. Conversely, a property proved at every inspected finite level does not automatically survive an infinite limit. The finite results therefore serve both as constructive building blocks and as necessary obstruction tests for any later extension.

\section{Derived quantities as finite row families}
\subsection{Baseline row families}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{derived\_\allowbreak{}quantities/baseline.py} provides the first rows computed from core objects: node/event genesis rows, projection grammar rows, carrier-state rows, orientation rows, and region/GNS rows.

\begin{proposition}[Baseline rows are evaluations of core constructors]
Every baseline row can be traced to one of: \code{EmptySetGrowthModel}, prefix push-forward, terminal carrier/state extraction, orientation measurement, regional Schur response, or finite GNS construction.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The object is the row family returned by the five public baseline functions. \textbf{Step 1.} \code{level\_\allowbreak{}models} enumerates finite approximants. \textbf{Step 2.} \code{genesis\_\allowbreak{}rows} reads node and event counts from the growth object. \textbf{Step 3.} \code{projection\_\allowbreak{}grammar\_\allowbreak{}rows} applies prefix push-forwards. \textbf{Step 4.} \code{carrier\_\allowbreak{}state\_\allowbreak{}rows} evaluates terminal distributions. \textbf{Step 5.} \code{orientation\_\allowbreak{}rows} and \code{region\_\allowbreak{}and\_\allowbreak{}gns\_\allowbreak{}rows} call signed-current, Schur, and algebra/GNS constructors. \textbf{Counter-check.} These rows add no independent object; mismatches identify a constructor or mapping defect. \textbf{Conclusion.} Baseline is the first complete finite evaluation layer.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Rank, kernel, coverage, and compressed projectivity}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{rank\_\allowbreak{}kernel\_\allowbreak{}conditions.py} measures local response-density rank/kernel behavior and construction coverage. \code{compressed\_\allowbreak{}state\_\allowbreak{}projection\_\allowbreak{}consistency.py} measures whether compressed carrier states remain projective under target-level truncation.

\begin{proposition}[Compressed projectivity check]
For target levels \(k<\ell\), the projectivity rows compare a direct compression at level \(k\) against the push-forward of a compression at level \(\ell\).
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The object is a pair of compressed distributions over role/address keys. \textbf{Step 1.} Build the compressed state at the finer target level. \textbf{Step 2.} Push it forward by address truncation. \textbf{Step 3.} Build the coarser compressed state directly. \textbf{Step 4.} Compare them by total variation and sector profiles. \textbf{Counter-check.} A non-zero discrepancy can arise from role/coarse-key mismatch and is recorded as a finite discrepancy, not as a continuum anomaly. \textbf{Conclusion.} The check decides finite compatibility of the implemented compression map.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Structural live-record ledgers}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{structural\_\allowbreak{}ledgers.py} records parameter purity, live-record structural calculations, history deltas, carrier effects, and state-sector decompositions.

\begin{proposition}[Live-record history delta uses one generated approximant]
The history-delta rows are computed from live and record states generated by the same \(b,L,m\) approximant; no additional state is fitted.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The object is the paired row family over \(\mathrm{live}\) and \(\mathrm{record}\). \textbf{Step 1.} Construct one growth model. \textbf{Step 2.} Read both edge states from that model. \textbf{Step 3.} Compute row differences and carrier effects. \textbf{Counter-check.} If a row depends on an external parameter not present in the model arguments, it is outside this construction route. \textbf{Conclusion.} The ledger is a bookkeeping layer over core state separation.
\end{proof}

\subsection{Interlevel operator maps and interface rows}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{operator\_\allowbreak{}stack\_\allowbreak{}residuals.py} builds interlevel rows for GNS generator maps, algebra projection maps, conditional-expectation proxies, finite log-density preflow rows, and composition rows. \code{operator\_\allowbreak{}stack\_\allowbreak{}interface\_\allowbreak{}residuals.py} builds seam, state-comparison, scaling, and map-naturality rows. \code{operator\_\allowbreak{}stack\_\allowbreak{}consistency\_\allowbreak{}checks.py} builds finite condition rows for role dimensions, generator ranks, coordinate changes, boundary-response model choice, and skew survival.

\begin{definition}[Interlevel residual]
Let $X_M$ be a finite object at source level $M$ and let $X_L$ be the corresponding object at target level $L<M$. When both objects are represented on a common finite label set, the row family records residuals of the form
\begin{equation}
  \rho_{M\to L}(X)=\frac{\|\Pi_{M\to L}(X_M)-X_L\|_F}{\max(\|X_L\|_F,\eps)}.
  \label{eq:interlevel-residual}
\end{equation}
Here $\Pi_{M\to L}$ is the label-span projection or prefix-induced map implemented by the row function that produces the row.
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Interlevel rows are finite discrepancies]
The interlevel rows record finite discrepancies between constructed objects at two levels. They do not construct an infinite-level object.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The source objects are the finite response-algebra, GNS, conditional-expectation, and log-density rows at two levels. \textbf{Step 1.} Construct the level-$M$ and level-$L$ rows from the same $b$, mode, state, region rule, and growth schedule. \textbf{Step 2.} Express the two rows in shared labels when such labels are present. \textbf{Step 3.} Evaluate Frobenius, relative, or scalar residuals. \textbf{Counter-check.} A non-zero row is retained as a finite discrepancy for that map and label route. \textbf{Conclusion.} The row family is a finite comparison layer over already constructed matrix objects.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Regenerated row command.}
The row values in this subsection are generated by the following command:
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg
PYTHONPATH=src python3 run_cnna.py \
  --branching 4 \
  --levels 4 5 \
  --modes linear \
  --states live record sym \
  --max-regions 2 \
  --max-boundary 700 \
  --max-interior 1000 \
  --directed-frontier-cap 6 \
  --algebra-tol 1e-10 \
  --algebra-max-iter 8 \
  --gns-tol 1e-10 \
  --growth-schedule layer_batch \
  --outdir outputs/transport_j_relation_reduced
\end{verbatim}

\subsection{Directed transport rows}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{directed\_\allowbreak{}current\_\allowbreak{}transport.py} builds the boundary-prefix transport matrix, transport rows, composition rows, orientation summary rows, and finite coverage rows. The transport matrix sends source boundary indicators to target prefix classes.

\begin{proposition}[Directed transport row]
For levels $M>L$, each directed transport row compares the direct level-$L$ directed response with the transported level-$M$ directed response after applying the finite address-prefix transport matrix.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The objects are two directed response matrices and the boundary-prefix transport matrix. \textbf{Step 1.} Build source and target boundary lists. \textbf{Step 2.} Build the finite transport matrix from address prefixes. \textbf{Step 3.} Push the source response to target coordinates. \textbf{Step 4.} Record direct-vs-transported discrepancies and scale-fit quantities. \textbf{Counter-check.} Boundary-role mismatch and open-grounded degrees can produce non-zero rows. \textbf{Conclusion.} The rows are finite transport comparisons between two constructed directed responses.
\end{proof}

\subsection{A/J orientation transport and composition}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{j\_\allowbreak{}orientation\_\allowbreak{}projectivity.py} builds A/J orientation snapshots, transport rows, composition rows, and a summary row. These rows use the skew part $A$ on the positive support selected by the symmetric part $S$.

\begin{definition}[A/J orientation row]
An A/J row contains a finite skew-plane object, its support data, and its transported or composed counterpart. The scalar errors compare the orientation after transport or composition with the orientation obtained by direct construction at the target level.
\end{definition}

\subsection{Local/global J sign relation}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{local\_\allowbreak{}global\_\allowbreak{}j\_\allowbreak{}sign\_\allowbreak{}relation.py} builds global boundary sign rows and compares them with local region sign rows. The rows compare relative signs only; the simultaneous global flip remains represented by a separate field.

\begin{proposition}[Local/global sign rows compare relative signs]
The local/global sign rows compare a local sign with a global boundary sign on the same finite construction route. The rows retain the simultaneous sign-flip field and do not select an absolute sign.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The objects are local finite J comparison rows and global boundary sign rows. \textbf{Step 1.} Construct global boundary sign data. \textbf{Step 2.} Construct local region sign data. \textbf{Step 3.} Compare the local sign to the global sign. \textbf{Step 4.} Evaluate the simultaneous flip field. \textbf{Counter-check.} A zero flip-invariance error records that the comparison is relative to a simultaneous sign flip. \textbf{Conclusion.} The row family represents finite relative sign data.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Regenerated row command.}
The row values in this subsection are generated by the following command:
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg
PYTHONPATH=src python3 run_local_global_j_sign_relation.py \
  --branching 4 \
  --levels 4 5 \
  --modes linear \
  --max-regions 2 \
  --max-boundary 700 \
  --max-interior 1000 \
  --directed-frontier-cap 6 \
  --gns-tol 1e-10 \
  --growth-schedule layer_batch \
  --outdir outputs/local_global_j_sign_relation
\end{verbatim}

\subsection{Live-record backreaction current}\label{sec:live-record-backreaction-current}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{backreaction\_\allowbreak{}current.py} constructs regional rows for the live-record current defined in Equation~\eqref{eq:live-record-backreaction-current}, where $A_{\mathrm{live},L}$ and $A_{\mathrm{record},L}$ are the skew parts of directed Schur responses built on the same branching value, level, mode, region, boundary response model, and matrix sign relation. The same module writes growth-profile, region-sensitivity, transport, and summary rows.

\begin{definition}[Live-record backreaction current]
For a fixed finite region $R$ and level $L$, let
\begin{equation}
\begin{aligned}
  A^{R}_{\mathrm{live},L}&=\frac{M^{R}_{\mathrm{live},L}-(M^{R}_{\mathrm{live},L})^T}{2},\\
  A^{R}_{\mathrm{record},L}&=\frac{M^{R}_{\mathrm{record},L}-(M^{R}_{\mathrm{record},L})^T}{2}.
\end{aligned}
\label{eq:live-record-skew-parts}
\end{equation}
The live-record backreaction current is the finite skew matrix
\begin{equation}
  B^{R}_L=A^{R}_{\mathrm{live},L}-A^{R}_{\mathrm{record},L}.
  \label{eq:live-record-backreaction-current}
\end{equation}
Three distinct scalar readouts are used and must not be identified:
\begin{equation}
  b_L^R=\|B_L^R\|_F,\qquad
  \beta_L^R=\|A^{R}_{\mathrm{live},L}\|_F-\|A^{R}_{\mathrm{record},L}\|_F,\qquad
  \omega_L^R=\langle F_1,B_L^R F_2\rangle.
  \label{eq:backreaction-scalar-readouts}
\end{equation}
Here \(b_L^R\ge0\) is the norm of the difference matrix, \(\beta_L^R\) is a signed difference of two norms, and \(\omega_L^R\) is the oriented scalar of the compressed F1/F2 plane. A zero crossing of \(\beta_L^R\) is neither a zero of \(B_L^R\) nor a sign change of \(\omega_L^R\).
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Backreaction rows compare live skew response with birth record]
The current $B^{R}_L$ in Equation~\eqref{eq:live-record-backreaction-current} is computed by subtracting the record skew response from the live skew response of Equation~\eqref{eq:live-record-skew-parts} on the same finite row coordinates.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The inputs are two directed Schur responses with identical finite model, region, boundary, and sign-relation fields. \textbf{Step 1.} Build the live directed response and extract its skew part. \textbf{Step 2.} Build the record directed response and extract its skew part. \textbf{Step 3.} Subtract the two skew matrices. \textbf{Step 4.} Evaluate the three separate readouts in Equation~\eqref{eq:backreaction-scalar-readouts}. \textbf{Recorded branch.} The sign of \(\beta_L^R\) compares skew magnitudes; the sign of \(\omega_L^R\) concerns the selected oriented plane. Neither is a matrix-order statement, and \(b_L^R\) is unsigned. \textbf{Conclusion.} The row represents a finite matrix difference between current network response and birth-record response together with explicitly separated scalar diagnostics.
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Regenerated row command.}
The following command generates the live-record backreaction surface over three terminal levels for the displayed row values:
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg
PYTHONPATH=src python3 run_live_record_backreaction_current.py \
  --branching 4 \
  --levels 4 5 6 \
  --modes linear \
  --max-regions 1 \
  --max-boundary 600 \
  --max-interior 900 \
  --directed-frontier-cap 6 \
  --growth-schedule layer_batch \
  --outdir outputs/live_record_backreaction_linear_L456_layer_batch
\end{verbatim}

\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.16\linewidth}>{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{0.16\linewidth}>{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{0.18\linewidth}>{\raggedleft\arraybackslash}p{0.18\linewidth}>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.24\linewidth}}

\subsection{Birth-relative backreaction rows}
\paragraph{Code map.} \code{backreaction\_\allowbreak{}current.py} now writes the sibling-root birth level explicitly from \code{RegionSpec.left\_root} and verifies equality with the right sibling-root level. \code{backreaction\_\allowbreak{}birth\_\allowbreak{}relative.py} reads that field, attaches a birth-relative age, and computes zero-crossing rows from the signed norm-difference sequence \(\beta_L^R\). The age used for the law rows is
\begin{equation}
  \alpha=L_{\mathrm{frontier}}-L_{\mathrm{birth}}.
  \label{eq:birth-relative-age}
\end{equation}
Rows using coarse-frontier coverage are kept out of the law fit by the row fields.

\begin{proposition}[Birth-relative crossing row]
For one branching value, mode, and region index, the zero-crossing row is obtained from a finite list of signed scalar backreaction rows ordered by birth-relative age.
\end{proposition}
\begin{proof}
\textbf{Object.} The input is the finite row list containing \(B_L^R\) and its scalar readouts for one region family. \textbf{Step 1.} Read the explicit sibling-root birth level and attach the age in Equation~\eqref{eq:birth-relative-age}. \textbf{Step 2.} Sort the eligible full-terminal rows by \(\alpha\). \textbf{Step 3.} Read \(\beta_L^R=\code{live\_minus\_record\_skew\_norm}\). \textbf{Step 4.} If two adjacent ages carry opposite signs, record the first sign change and linearly interpolate the two sampled \(\beta\)-values. \textbf{Recorded branch.} If the sampled sequence contains no opposite signs, the crossing fields remain empty. \textbf{Conclusion.} The row is a finite interpolation of the skew-norm difference \(\beta_L^R\); it is not a zero of the matrix current \(B_L^R\), whose Frobenius norm remains non-negative, and it is not a crossing of \(\omega_L^R\).
\end{proof}

\paragraph{Regenerated row command.}
The following command generates the birth-relative rows displayed below:
\begin{verbatim}
cd anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg
PYTHONPATH=src python3 run_birth_relative_backreaction_current.py \
  --branching 4 \
  --levels 4 5 6 \
  --modes linear \
  --max-regions 1 \
  --max-boundary 600 \
  --max-interior 900 \
  --directed-frontier-cap 6 \
  --growth-schedule layer_batch \
  --outdir outputs/birth_relative_backreaction_linear_L456_layer_batch
\end{verbatim}

A schedule-stability value for the interpolated \(\beta\)-crossing is not stated here.

\newpage

\section{Conclusion}
The finite core developed here provides an explicitly checkable base layer for CNNA. It begins from an empty finite configuration and introduces no external geometric background, continuum space, spacetime, field variables, integrals, or differential operators. The rooted $b$-ary tree is used as a finite provenance carrier, not as a discretized physical geometry; a Sierpi\'nski-type realization is not part of the finite derivation. Its subsequent objects are generated by a declared deterministic rule family. The branching index \(b\), depth cutoff \(L\), mode selector \(m\), update schedule \(\mathfrak{s}\), readout selectors, caps, and numerical tolerances are separated rather than collapsed into a claim of derivation from the empty configuration alone. This makes the model transparent in the finite sense: growth histories, edge weights, carrier push-forwards, response maps, algebraic closures, \(J\)-comparisons, local operator diagnostics, and backreaction rows can all be traced to finite combinatorial and matrix operations with explicit inputs.

The main value of the finite formulation is therefore not that it is already the most general or final form of the theory. Its value is that it displays the generative skeleton before further structures are placed on top of it. In a more developed formulation, one expects additional layers: larger region systems, deeper histories, refined operator surfaces, transport structures, limiting procedures, and eventually infinite or completed objects. Those layers may become mathematically more expressive, but they also make it harder to see which parts are genuinely derived and which parts have been added by representation. The present finite core is designed to keep that distinction visible.

The finite source now separates exact results from numerical observations. For fixed admissible inputs the growth object is well-defined; the symmetric open-grounded Schur response is positive semidefinite under its stated hypotheses; the finite real GNS quotient is a cyclic transpose representation; simultaneous orientation reversal leaves all sign-even finite-\(J\) diagnostics unchanged; the backreaction matrix and its scalar readouts obey explicit non-implications; address/carrier handoff composes exactly; and a linearly growing address-contrast carrier admits exact non-unital corner embeddings. Directed live and record surfaces still differ from the symmetrized control, skew components survive controlled decompositions, and local/operator transport diagnostics expose both satisfied and failed stronger properties. The first group consists of finite theorems. The remaining row statements are finite observations with residuals, not continuum theorems.

The discrete Dirichlet-to-Neumann construction is especially important in the finite sense used here. The DtN-type maps are Schur-complement response matrices on finite generated graphs. Boundary sets, interior sets, invertibility conditions, residuals, and compressed responses are explicit and directly inspectable. The complete finite provenance is now stated without a semantic gap: a birth adds a set-theoretic and coordinate-linear complement to the existing carrier; the generated directed weights couple that new direction to the prior network; a selected boundary/interior cut produces a directed Schur response; and only that response is decomposed into symmetric and skew parts. The complement identity by itself does not imply a skew sector. The construction is therefore a finite algebraic network response calculation, not an imported analytic boundary theory.

The conclusion is therefore conservative. The finite CNNA core demonstrates a coherent, reproducible discrete model initialized by an empty configuration and evolved by an explicit rule family. In comparisons where \(m\), \(\mathfrak{s}\), region selection, caps, and tolerances are fixed, \(b\) is the varied structural index and \(L\) the finite cutoff. The model supplies a controlled response calculus and a nontrivial hierarchy of finite diagnostics, but it does not establish an infinite theory or equivalence with established continuum frameworks. It provides a transparent base from which higher structures can be formulated without disguising fixed rules or numerical selectors as derived quantities.

\section{Outlook}
The next conceptual step is not to abandon the finite model, but to use it as the visible base layer for more structured extensions. The finite core should be understood as the simplest representation of the theory, not as its endpoint. Its role is comparable to a coordinate chart for the generative mechanism: it makes the construction inspectable before more complex objects, completions, or limiting procedures are introduced.

The first nontrivial carrier step has now been completed at finite level. The first-order address-contrast spaces grow with depth, intertwine exactly under the derived \code{fiber\_sqrt} prefix map, and yield composable non-unital corner embeddings of real full matrix algebras. This closes the carrier-algebra part of the handoff without pretending that the distinguished data are already natural. The independently reconstructed responses and densities drift under the corner restriction, the corresponding independent GNS metrics are not isometric, and the finite-\(J\) objects both fail to intertwine and leak into newly added role directions. The next structural problem is therefore sharper than before: derive a live evolution or completely positive transport law for these distinguished objects, or identify a state construction compatible with the exact carrier embeddings. A norm completion and its physical interpretation remain separate from the finite results proved here.

A first direction is therefore the infinite extension. This should be treated as a higher intermediate objective, not as an automatic consequence of the finite rows. Passing from finite generated carriers and matrices to infinite or completed objects would require additional mathematical control: compatible growth embeddings, stability of carrier push-forwards, convergence or projective consistency of response maps, control of residuals under depth increase, and a precise account of which finite diagnostics survive the limit. In particular, the existence of finite \(J\)-objects, finite GNS-like structures, or finite local operator checks should not by itself be read as the existence of corresponding infinite objects. Those claims require separate construction.

A second direction is deeper comparison with the discrete models separated in Section~\ref{sec:related-work}. The current comparison distinguishes carrier growth from occupation growth, sequential from synchronous event order, preservation from rearrangement of prior genealogy, forward response from inverse reconstruction, and finite combinatorial networks from metric quantum graphs. Future work should sharpen those bridges by proving explicit correspondences or incompatibility results, not by relying on shared terminology. The present model still introduces no manifold, smooth function, measure, integral, differential operator, or analytic boundary value problem. Likewise, a tree--Sierpi\'nski correspondence is not assumed: it remains a separate finite gluing or duality problem.

A third direction is structural enrichment above the finite core. The present code exposes live, record, and sym surfaces, finite boundary responses, directed decompositions, local operator diagnostics, sign transport, and backreaction currents. Higher layers could add more refined region systems, larger multi-scale carrier hierarchies, stronger consistency constraints between local and global objects, categorical or functorial descriptions of growth and projection, or completion procedures that preserve the derived-only character of the construction. The key constraint should remain that no new primitive is introduced silently. If an additional object is added, its status should be explicit: derived finite object, diagnostic row, limiting candidate, external comparison structure, or genuine new axiom.

The long-term theoretical question is whether this empty-initialized finite hierarchy can support a controlled path toward richer mathematical structures without losing its auditability. The finite core shows that substantial response, orientation, algebraic, and backreaction structure can arise after fixing an explicit deterministic rule family. Future work must determine which features are stable under changes of \(m\), \(\mathfrak{s}\), region system, and cutoff, rather than assuming those selectors are irrelevant. An infinite extension would then test whether compatible objects can be stabilized beyond finite depth. Connections to established theories become meaningful only after the relevant bridges have been built: finite-to-infinite consistency, response-map convergence, operator-domain control, and preservation of the live/record distinction.

Thus the outlook is intentionally cautious. The present work does not claim that the finite model already is a completed infinite theory or a final higher-level structure. It claims something narrower and testable: before such higher structures are invoked, there exists a reproducible discrete--finite CNNA core with an empty initial configuration, an explicit deterministic rule family, and declared finite selectors, in which the main objects can be computed, inspected, and challenged directly.

The complete Python source boundary documented in this article is included as arXiv ancillary material under \src{anc/CNNA_structured_core_pkg}. Generative AI assistance was used for drafting, review, code inspection, and documentation support. The author is solely responsible for the mathematical content, the implementation, the reported claims, and their interpretation.

Historical primary sources are retained where they are mathematically foundational; recent references are used to locate the finite construction within current tree-growth and network-response work. They do not supply primitives for the CNNA construction.

\section{Source-tree object boundary}
The Python source constructs finite objects at fixed values of $b$, $L$, mode, state, region selectors, finite caps, and tolerances. The constructed objects include finite trees with explicit open slots, finite growth-step records, finite edge dictionaries, finite distributions, finite Schur responses, finite directed responses, finite matrix algebras, finite GNS support quotients, finite transports, finite sign comparisons, finite backreaction-current rows, and row-family tables computed from those objects.

\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.28\linewidth}>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.64\linewidth}}
\caption{Objects constructed by the inspected source tree.}\label{tab:constructed-objects}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Object group} & \textbf{Constructed finite objects}\\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Object group} & \textbf{Constructed finite objects}\\
\midrule
\endhead
Growth and schedule & \code{Node}, \code{Event}, \code{OpenBirthSlot}, \code{GrowthStep}, \code{EmptySetGrowthModel}, record/live edge dictionaries, and schedule-dependent generated rows.\\
\hline
Carrier and prefix maps & terminal address sets, uniform carrier, terminal diagonal state, prefix push-forward, center/non-center split, finite total-variation rows, nested address-contrast role carriers, label inclusions, and non-unital corner embeddings.\\
\hline
Response matrices & symmetric Schur--DtN response matrices, directed Schur response matrices, symmetric/skew decomposition, role-compressed response matrices, and finite residual fields.\\
\hline
Finite algebra layer & finite matrix-basis closure, positive density from response support, finite Gram matrices, tolerance-resolved quotient support, full-basis left-action audits, explicit cyclic-span ranks, and finite GNS data.\\
\hline
Regional/local layer & regional response records, local generator systems, support projectors, restricted finite algebras, joined algebras, commutator matrices, finite commutants, Tomita star-map matrices, and split residual rows.\\
\hline
State and entropy rows & compressed carrier/GNS-state objects, refined boundary-role bases, finite entropy, finite relative entropy, Jensen--Shannon divergence, and record/live entropy rows.\\
\hline
Transport and orientation rows & interlevel projection residuals, seam/interface rows, directed-current transport, A/J orientation snapshots, orientation transport, local/global sign comparisons, and finite sign summaries.\\
\hline
Backreaction rows & live/record skew-response matrix $B_L=A_L^{\rm live}-A_L^{\rm record}$, its norm $b_L$, skew-norm difference $\beta_L$, oriented scalar $\omega_L$, transported rows, explicit sibling-root birth levels, $\beta$-crossing rows, and branching comparisons.\\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}

\begin{longtable}{>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.30\linewidth}>{\raggedright\arraybackslash}p{0.62\linewidth}}
\caption{Objects absent from the inspected source tree.}\label{tab:absent-objects}\\
\toprule
\textbf{Object name} & \textbf{Status in this source tree}\\
\midrule
\endfirsthead
\toprule
\textbf{Object name} & \textbf{Status in this source tree}\\
\midrule
\endhead
Infinite-level object & The source constructs a finite directed system of growing carrier algebras and corner embeddings, but no norm completion, inductive-limit object, or projective-limit object over all $L$ is present.\\
\hline
Von Neumann algebra closure & The source constructs finite matrix algebras and finite commutant bases; it contains no weak-operator or strong-operator closure.\\
\hline
Type-III result & The source contains finite rank, nullity, support, and residual rows; it contains no Type-III classification object.\\
\hline
Continuum spacetime model & The source contains finite growth steps, finite responses, and finite transport rows; it contains no continuum metric field.\\
\hline
Lorentz signature or local eigen-time & The source records causal depth, linearization index, and response/lapse weights; it contains no Lorentzian tangent structure or eigen-time field.\\
\hline
Unique global complex orientation & The source contains finite local and global sign-comparison rows; it contains no constructor selecting one absolute global sign of $J$.\\
\hline
Asymptotic theorem over $L$ & The source emits rows at specified finite levels; it contains no theorem over all $L$ or a convergence proof.\\
\bottomrule
\end{longtable}
